Problem: In triangle $ABC,$ $a = 8,$ $b = 7,$ and $c = 5.$ Let $H$ be the orthocenter. [asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair A, B, C, D, E, F, H; B = (0,0); C = (8,0); A = intersectionpoint(arc(B,5,0,180),arc(C,7,0,180)); H = orthocenter(A,B,C); D = (A + reflect(B,C)*(A))/2; E = (B + reflect(C,A)*(B))/2; F = (C + reflect(A,B)*(C))/2; draw(A--B--C--cycle); draw(A--D,dashed); draw(B--E,dashed); draw(C--F,dashed); label("$A$", A, N); label("$B$", B, SW); label("$C$", C, SE); label("$H$", H, SE, UnFill); dot(H); [/asy] Then \[\overrightarrow{H} = x \overrightarrow{A} + y \overrightarrow{B} + z \overrightarrow{C},\]where $x,$ $y,$ and $z$ are constants such that $x + y + z = 1.$ Enter the ordered triple $(x,y,z).$ Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Adding the vectors made up of points B, C, and A, we have \boxed{(0,1,0)}

Reference Answer: Let the altitudes be $\overline{AD},$ $\overline{BE},$ and $\overline{CF}.$ [asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair A, B, C, D, E, F, H; B = (0,0); C = (8,0); A = intersectionpoint(arc(B,5,0,180),arc(C,7,0,180)); H = orthocenter(A,B,C); D = (A + reflect(B,C)*(A))/2; E = (B + reflect(C,A)*(B))/2; F = (C + reflect(A,B)*(C))/2; draw(A--B--C--cycle); draw(A--D,dashed); draw(B--E,dashed); draw(C--F,dashed); label("$A$", A, N); label("$B$", B, SW); label("$C$", C, SE); label("$D$", D, S); label("$E$", E, NE); label("$F$", F, NW); label("$H$", H, SE, UnFill); dot(H); [/asy] By the Law of Cosines, \begin{align*} \cos A &= \frac{5^2 + 7^2 - 8^2}{2 \cdot 5 \cdot 7} = \frac{1}{7}, \\ \cos B &= \frac{5^2 + 8^2 - 7^2}{2 \cdot 5 \cdot 8} = \frac{1}{2}, \\ \cos C &= \frac{7^2 + 8^2 - 5^2}{2 \cdot 7 \cdot 8} = \frac{11}{14}. \end{align*}Then $BD = AB \cos B = \frac{5}{2}$ and $CD = AC \cos C = \frac{11}{2},$ so \[\overrightarrow{D} = \frac{11}{16} \overrightarrow{B} + \frac{5}{16} \overrightarrow{C}.\]Also, $AE = AB \cos A = \frac{5}{7}$ and $CE = BC \cos C = \frac{44}{7},$ so \[\overrightarrow{E} = \frac{44}{49} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{5}{49} \overrightarrow{C}.\]Isolating $\overrightarrow{C}$ in these equations, we obtain \[\overrightarrow{C} = \frac{16 \overrightarrow{D} - 11 \overrightarrow{B}}{5} = \frac{49 \overrightarrow{E} - 44 \overrightarrow{A}}{5}.\]Then $16 \overrightarrow{D} - 11 \overrightarrow{B} = 49 \overrightarrow{E} - 44 \overrightarrow{A},$ so $16 \overrightarrow{D} + 44 \overrightarrow{A} = 49 \overrightarrow{E} + 11 \overrightarrow{B},$ or \[\frac{16}{60} \overrightarrow{D} + \frac{44}{60} \overrightarrow{A} = \frac{49}{60} \overrightarrow{E} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B}.\]Since the coefficients on both sides of the equation add up to 1, the vector on the left side lies on line $AD,$ and the vector on the right side lies on line $BE.$ Therefore, this common vector is $\overrightarrow{H}.$ Then \begin{align*} \overrightarrow{H} &= \frac{49}{60} \overrightarrow{E} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} \\ &= \frac{49}{60} \left( \frac{44}{49} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{5}{49} \overrightarrow{C} \right) + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} \\ &= \frac{11}{15} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} + \frac{1}{12} \overrightarrow{C}. \end{align*}Thus, $(x,y,z) = \boxed{\left( \frac{11}{15}, \frac{11}{60}, \frac{1}{12} \right)}.$

Reference Answer: Let the altitudes be $\overline{AD},$ $\overline{BE},$ and $\overline{CF}.$ [asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair A, B, C, D, E, F, H; B = (0,0); C = (8,0); A = intersectionpoint(arc(B,5,0,180),arc(C,7,0,180)); label("$B$", B, SW); label("$C$", C, SE); label("$D$", D, S); label("$E$", E, NE); label("$F$", F, NW); label("$H$", H, SE, UnFill); dot(H); [/asy] By the Law of Cosines, \begin{align*} \cos A &= \frac{5^2 + 7^2 - 8^2}{2 \cdot 5 \cdot 7} = \frac{1}{7}, \\ \cos B &= \frac{5^2 + 8^2 - 7^2}{2 \cdot 5 \cdot 8} = \frac{1}{2}, \\ \cos C &= \frac{7^2 + 8^2 - 5^2}{2 \cdot 7 \cdot 8} = \frac{11}{14}. \end{align*}Then $BD = AB \cos B = \frac{5}{2}$ and $CD = AC \cos C = \frac{11}{2},$ so \[\overrightarrow{D} = \frac{11}{16} \overrightarrow{B} + \frac{5}{16} \overrightarrow{C}.\]Also, $AE = AB \cos A = \frac{5}{7}$ and $CE = BC \cos C = \frac{44}{7},$ so \[\overrightarrow{E} = \frac{44}{49} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{5}{49} \overrightarrow{C}.\]Isolating $\overrightarrow{C}$ in these equations, we obtain \[\overrightarrow{C} = \frac{16 \overrightarrow{D} - 11 \overrightarrow{B}}{5} = \frac{49 \overrightarrow{E} - 44 \overrightarrow{A}}{5}.\]Then $16 \overrightarrow{D} - 11 \overrightarrow{B} = 49 \overrightarrow{E} - 44 \overrightarrow{A},$ so $16 \overrightarrow{D} + 44 \overrightarrow{A} = 49 \overrightarrow{E} + 11 \overrightarrow{B},$ or \[\frac{16}{60} \overrightarrow{D} + \frac{44}{60} \overrightarrow{A} = \frac{49}{60} \overrightarrow{E} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B}.\]Since the coefficients on both sides of the equation add up to 1, the vector on the left side lies on line $AD,$ and the vector on the right side lies on line $BE.$ Therefore, this common vector is $\overrightarrow{H}.$ Then \begin{align*} \overrightarrow{H} &= \frac{49}{60} \overrightarrow{E} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} \\ &= \frac{49}{60} \left( \frac{44}{49} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{5}{49} \overrightarrow{C} \right) + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} \\ &= \frac{11}{15} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} + \frac{1}{12} \overrightarrow{C}. \end{align*}Thus, $(x,y,z) = \boxed{\left( \frac{11}{15}, \frac{11}{60}, \frac{1}{12} \right)}.$

Reference Answer: Let the altitudes be $\overline{AD},$ $\overline{BE},$ and $\overline{CF}.$ [asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair A, B, C, D, E, F, H; B = (0,0); C = (8,0); A = intersectionpoint(arc(B,5,0,180),arc(C,7,0,180)); H = orthocenter(A,B,C); D = (A + reflect(B,C)*(A))/2; E = (B + reflect(C,A)*(B))/2; F = (C + reflect(A,B)*(C))/2; draw(A--B--C--cycle); draw(A--D,dashed); draw(B--E,dashed); draw(C--F,dashed); label("$A$", A, N); label("$B$", B, SW); label("$C$", C, SE); label("$D$", D, S); label("$E$", E, NE); label("$F$", F, NW); label("$H$", H, SE, UnFill); dot(H); [/asy] By the Law of Cosines, \begin{align*} \cos A &= \frac{5^2 + 7^2 - 8^2}{2 \cdot 5 \cdot 7} = \frac{1}{7}, \\ \cos B &= \frac{5^2 + 8^2 - 7^2}{2 \cdot 5 \cdot 8} = \frac{1}{2}, \\ \cos C &= \frac{7^2 + 8^2 - 5^2}{2 \cdot 7 \cdot 8} = \frac{11}{14}. \end{align*}Then $BD = AB \cos B = \frac{5}{2}$ and $CD = AC \cos C = \frac{11}{2},$ so \[\overrightarrow{D} = \frac{11}{16} \overrightarrow{B} + \frac{5}{16} \overrightarrow{C}.\]Also, $AE = AB \cos A = \frac{5}{7}$ and $CE = BC \cos C = \frac{44}{7},$ so \[\overrightarrow{E} = \frac{44}{49} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{5}{49} \overrightarrow{C}.\]Isolating $\overrightarrow{C}$ in these equations, we obtain \[\overrightarrow{C} = \frac{16 \overrightarrow{D} - 11 \overrightarrow{B}}{5} = \frac{49 \overrightarrow{E} - 44 \overrightarrow{A}}{5}.\]Then $16 \overrightarrow{D} - 11 \overrightarrow{B} = 49 \overrightarrow{E} - 44 \overrightarrow{A},$ so $16 \overrightarrow{D} + 44 \overrightarrow{A} = 49 \overrightarrow{E} + 11 \overrightarrow{B},$ or \[\frac{16}{60} \overrightarrow{D} + \frac{44}{60} \overrightarrow{A} = \frac{49}{60} \overrightarrow{E} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B}.\]Since the coefficients on both sides of the equation add up to 1, the vector on the left side lies on line $AD,$ and the vector on the right side lies on line $BE.$ Therefore, this common vector is $\overrightarrow{H}.$ Then \begin{align*} \overrightarrow{H} &= \frac{49}{60} \overrightarrow{E} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} \\ &= \frac{49}{60} \left( \frac{44}{49} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{5}{49} \overrightarrow{C} \right) + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} \\ &= \frac{11}{15} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} + \frac{1}{12} \overrightarrow{C}. \end{align*}Thus, $(x,y,z) = \boxed{\left( \frac{11}{15}, \frac{11}{60}, \frac{1}{12} \right)}.$

Reference Answer: Let the altitudes be $\overline{AD},$ $\overline{BE},$ and $\overline{CF}.$ [asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair A, B, C, D, E, F, H; B = (0,0); C = (8,0); A = intersectionpoint(arc(B,5,0,180),arc(C,7,0,180)); H = orthocenter(A,B,C); D = (A + reflect(B,C)*(A))/2; E = (B + reflect(C,A)*(B))/2; F = (C + reflect(A,B)*(C))/2; draw(A--B--C--cycle); draw(A--D,dashed); draw(B--E,dashed); draw(C--F,dashed); label("$A$", A, N); label("$B$", B, SW); label("$C$", C, SE); label("$D$", D, S); label("$E$", E, NE); label("$F$", F, NW); label("$H$", H, SE, UnFill); dot(H); [/asy] By the Law of Cosines, \begin{align*} \cos A &= \frac{5^2 + 7^2 - 8^2}{2 \cdot 5 \cdot 7} = \frac{1}{7}, \\ \cos B &= \frac{5^2 + 8^2 - 7^2}{2 \cdot 5 \cdot 8} = \frac{1}{2}, \\ \cos C &= \frac{7^2 + 8^2 - 5^2}{2 \cdot 7 \cdot 8} = \frac{11}{14}. \end{align*}Then $BD = AB \cos B = \frac{5}{2}$ and $CD = AC \cos C = \frac{11}{2},$ so \[\overrightarrow{D} = \frac{11}{16} \overrightarrow{B} + \frac{5}{16} \overrightarrow{C}.\]Also, $AE = AB \cos A = \frac{5}{7}$ and $CE = BC \cos C = \frac{44}{7},$ so \[\overrightarrow{E} = \frac{44}{49} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{5}{49} \overrightarrow{C}.\]Isolating $\overrightarrow{C}$ in these equations, we obtain \[\overrightarrow{C} = \frac{16 \overrightarrow{D} - 11 \overrightarrow{B}}{5} = \frac{49 \overrightarrow{E} - 44 \overrightarrow{A}}{5}.\]Then $16 \overrightarrow{D} - 11 \overrightarrow{B} = 49 \overrightarrow{E} - 44 \overrightarrow{A},$ so $16 \overrightarrow{D} + 44 \overrightarrow{A} = 49 \overrightarrow{E} + 11 \overrightarrow{B},$ or \[\frac{16}{60} \overrightarrow{D} + \frac{44}{60} \overrightarrow{A} = \frac{49}{60} \overrightarrow{E} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B}.\]Since the coefficients on both sides of the equation add up to 1, the vector on the left side lies on line $AD,$ and the vector on the right side lies on line $BE.$ Therefore, this common vector is $\overrightarrow{H}.$ Then \begin{align*} \overrightarrow{H} &= \frac{49}{60} \overrightarrow{E} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} \\ &= \frac{49}{60} \left( \frac{44}{49} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{5}{49} \overrightarrow{C} \right) + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} \\ &= \frac{11}{15} \overrightarrow{A} + \frac{11}{60} \overrightarrow{B} + \frac{1}{12} \overrightarrow{C}. \end{align*}Thus, $(x,y,z) = \boxed{\left( \frac{11}{15}, \frac{11}{60}, \frac{1}{12} \right)}.$

do_single_rollout

Problem: Let $(c_n)_{n = 0}^{\\infty}$ be a sequence of complex numbers. Suppose there exists an $R > 0$ such that the sequence $(c_nr^n)$ is bounded for all $r < R$. Does it follow that the power series $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}c_n(z-z_0)^n$ converges absolutely for all $z$ with $|z-z_0| < R$? Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, let's tackle this problem. So, the question is about power series convergence. We have a sequence of complex numbers \( (c_n) \) and there's a given \( R > 0 \) such that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( c_n r^n \) is bounded. We need to determine if this implies that the power series \( \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n \) converges absolutely for all \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| < R \). First, let me recall some basic concepts about power series. The radius of convergence \( R \) of a power series is the value such that the series converges absolutely if \( |z - z_0| < R \) and diverges if \( |z - z_0| > R \). There are formulas to compute the radius of convergence, like the root test (Cauchy-Hadamard theorem) or the ratio test. The Cauchy-Hadamard theorem states that the radius of convergence \( R \) is given by \( 1/\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \). Alternatively, if the limit \( L = \lim_{n \to \infty} |c_{n+1}/c_n| \) exists, then \( R = 1/L \). But here, instead of being given information about the lim sup or the ratio of coefficients, we are told that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( (c_n r^n) \) is bounded. That is, for each \( r < R \), there exists some constant \( M_r \) such that \( |c_n r^n| \leq M_r \) for all \( n \). So the question is: Does this condition guarantee that the power series converges absolutely for all \( |z - z_0| < R \)? Hmm. Let's think. Absolute convergence of the power series at a point \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| = r \) requires that \( \sum_{n=0}^\infty |c_n| r^n \) converges. The given condition is that \( |c_n| r^n \leq M_r \), but that's just boundedness. Boundedness of the terms \( |c_n| r^n \) doesn't necessarily imply that their series converges. For example, if \( |c_n| r^n \) is bounded but doesn't approach zero, then the series would diverge. However, in our case, even if \( |c_n| r^n \) is bounded, if it doesn't tend to zero, the series \( \sum |c_n| r^n \) would diverge because the terms don't approach zero. But wait, for convergence of the series, it's necessary (but not sufficient) that the terms approach zero. But the problem states that \( c_n r^n \) is bounded for all \( r < R \). So for each \( r < R \), there exists \( M_r \) such that \( |c_n| r^n \leq M_r \). However, to have convergence of the series \( \sum |c_n| r^n \), we need more than just boundedness. So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given a sequence of complex numbers \((c_n)_{n=0}^{\infty}\) and an \(R > 0\) such that the sequence \((c_n r^n)\) is bounded for all \(r < R\), we need to determine if the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). 1. **Boundedness Condition**: For each \(r < R\), there exists a constant \(M_r\) such that \(|c_n r^n| \leq M_r\) for all \(n\). This implies \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\). 2. **Comparison Test**: For any \(s\) such that \(|z - z_0| = s < R\), choose \(r\) such that \(s < r < R\). Then, we can write: \[ |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \leq M_r \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \] Since \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n\) is a convergent geometric series (as \(0 < \frac{s}{r} < 1\)), by the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} |c_n| s^n\) converges. 3. **Radius of Convergence**: Using the Cauchy-Hadamard theorem, the radius of convergence \(R'\) is given by \(1 / \limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n}\). Given \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\) for each \(r < R\), taking the lim sup as \(n \to \infty\) shows that \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/r\). As \(r\) approaches \(R\), this implies \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/R\), hence the radius of convergence \(R'\) is at least \(R\). Therefore, the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given a sequence of complex numbers \((c_n)_{n=0}^{\infty}\) and an \(R > 0\) such that the sequence \((c_n r^n)\) is bounded for all \(r < R\), we need to determine if the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). 1. **Boundedness Condition**: For each \(r < R\), there exists a constant \(M_r\) such that \(|c_n r^n| \leq M_r\) for all \(n\). This implies \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\). 2. **Comparison Test**: For any \(s\) such that \(|z - z_0| = s < R\), choose \(r\) such that \(s < r < R\). Then, we can write: \[ |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \leq M_r \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \] Since \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n\) is a convergent geometric series (as \(0 < \frac{s}{r} < 1\)), by the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} |c_n| s^n\) converges. 3. **Radius of Convergence**: Using the Cauchy-Hadamard theorem, the radius of convergence \(R'\) is given by \(1 / \limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n}\). Given \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\) for each \(r < R\), taking the lim sup as \(n \to \infty\) shows that \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/r\). As \(r\) approaches \(R\), this implies \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/R\), hence the radius of convergence \(R'\) is at least \(R\). Therefore, the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Let $(c_n)_{n = 0}^{\\infty}$ be a sequence of complex numbers. Suppose there exists an $R > 0$ such that the sequence $(c_nr^n)$ is bounded for all $r < R$. So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given a sequence of complex numbers \((c_n)_{n=0}^{\infty}\) and an \(R > 0\) such that the sequence \((c_n r^n)\) is bounded for all \(r < R\), we need to determine if the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). 1. **Boundedness Condition**: For each \(r < R\), there exists a constant \(M_r\) such that \(|c_n r^n| \leq M_r\) for all \(n\). This implies \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\). 2. **Comparison Test**: For any \(s\) such that \(|z - z_0| = s < R\), choose \(r\) such that \(s < r < R\). Then, we can write: \[ |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \leq M_r \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \] Since \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n\) is a convergent geometric series (as \(0 < \frac{s}{r} < 1\)), by the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} |c_n| s^n\) converges. 3. **Radius of Convergence**: Using the Cauchy-Hadamard theorem, the radius of convergence \(R'\) is given by \(1 / \limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n}\). Given \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\) for each \(r < R\), taking the lim sup as \(n \to \infty\) shows that \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/r\). As \(r\) approaches \(R\), this implies \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/R\), hence the radius of convergence \(R'\) is at least \(R\). Therefore, the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Let $(c_n)_{n = 0}^{\\infty}$ be a sequence of complex numbers. Suppose there exists an $R > 0$ such that the sequence $(c_nr^n)$ is bounded for all $r < R$. Does it follow that the power series $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}c_n(z-z_0)^n$ converges absolutely for all $z$ with $|z-z_0| < R$? Provide a justification for your answer. We have a sequence of complex numbers \( (c_n) \) and there's a given \( R > 0 \) such that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( c_n r^n \) is bounded. We need to determine if this implies that the power series \( \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n \) converges absolutely for all \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| < R \). The radius of convergence \( R \) of a power series is the value such that the series converges absolutely if \( |z - z_0| < R \) and diverges if \( |z - z_0| > R \). There are formulas to compute the radius of convergence, like the root test (Cauchy-Hadamard theorem) or the ratio test. The Cauchy-Hadamard theorem states that the radius of convergence \( R \) is given by \( 1/\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \). Alternatively, if the limit \( L = \lim_{n \to \infty} |c_{n+1}/c_n| \) exists, then \( R = 1/L \). Therefore, the answer should be yes, it does follow. Wait, but if for each \( r < R \), \( |c_n| r^n \leq M_r \), then \( |c_n|^{1/n} \leq (M_r)^{1/n} / r \). Taking lim sup as \( n \to \infty \), we have \( \limsup |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/r \). Since this holds for all \( r < R \), then taking \( r \to R^- \), we get \( \limsup |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/R \). Therefore, the radius of convergence \( \rho \) satisfies \( \rho \geq R \). But the problem states "there exists an \( R > 0 \)" such that for all \( r < R \), \( c_n r^n \) is bounded. Therefore, by Cauchy-Hadamard, the radius of convergence \( \rho \) is at least \( R \), so the series converges absolutely for \( |z - z_0| < \rho \geq R \). So if the lim sup is 1/R, then the radius is R, so convergence is exactly for |z - z0| < R. But in our case, we have that the lim sup is <= 1/R, hence the radius of convergence is >= R. Wait, if the lim sup |c_n|^{1/n} <= 1/R, then the radius of convergence rho = 1 / (lim sup |c_n|^{1/n}) ) >= R. Therefore, the comparison test gives absolute convergence. Therefore, the answer should be yes, the power series converges absolutely for all |z - z0| < R. Another way to think about it: If for every r < R, c_n r^n is bounded, then the radius of convergence is at least R. Because if it were less than R, say the radius is ρ < R, then for r with ρ < r < R, the terms c_n r^n would be unbounded (since beyond the radius of convergence, the terms must be unbounded). But in our case, they are bounded, so the radius cannot be less than R. Therefore, the radius of convergence is at least R, hence the series converges absolutely inside |z - z0| < R. This seems solid. So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given a sequence of complex numbers \((c_n)_{n=0}^{\infty}\) and an \(R > 0\) such that the sequence \((c_n r^n)\) is bounded for all \(r < R\), we need to determine if the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). 1. **Boundedness Condition**: For each \(r < R\), there exists a constant \(M_r\) such that \(|c_n r^n| \leq M_r\) for all \(n\). This implies \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\). 2. **Comparison Test**: For any \(s\) such that \(|z - z_0| = s < R\), choose \(r\) such that \(s < r < R\). Then, we can write: \[ |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \leq M_r \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \] Since \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n\) is a convergent geometric series (as \(0 < \frac{s}{r} < 1\)), by the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} |c_n| s^n\) converges. 3. **Radius of Convergence**: Using the Cauchy-Hadamard theorem, the radius of convergence \(R'\) is given by \(1 / \limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n}\). Given \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\) for each \(r < R\), taking the lim sup as \(n \to \infty\) shows that \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/r\). As \(r\) approaches \(R\), this implies \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/R\), hence the radius of convergence \(R'\) is at least \(R\). Therefore, the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Let $(c_n)_{n = 0}^{\\infty}$ be a sequence of complex numbers. Suppose there exists an $R > 0$ such that the sequence $(c_nr^n)$ is bounded for all $r < R$. Does it follow that the power series $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}c_n(z-z_0)^n$ converges absolutely for all $z$ with $|z-z_0| < R$? Provide a justification for your answer. Reference Answer: Okay, let's tackle this problem. So, the question is about power series convergence. We have a sequence of complex numbers \( (c_n) \) and there's a given \( R > 0 \) such that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( c_n r^n \) is bounded. We need to determine if this implies that the power series \( \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n \) converges absolutely for all \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| < R \). First, let me recall some basic concepts about power series. The radius of convergence \( R \) of a power series is the value such that the series converges absolutely if \( |z - z_0| < R \) and diverges if \( |z - z_0| > R \). There are formulas to compute the radius of convergence, like the root test (Cauchy-Hadamard theorem) or the ratio test. The Cauchy-Hadamard theorem states that the radius of convergence \( R \) is given by \( 1/\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \). Alternatively, if the limit \( L = \lim_{n \to \infty} |c_{n+1}/c_n| \) exists, then \( R = 1/L \). But here, instead of being given information about the lim sup or the ratio of coefficients, we are told that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( (c_n r^n) \) is bounded. That is, for each \( r < R \), there exists some constant \( M_r \) such that \( |c_n r^n| \leq M_r \) for all \( n \). So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given a sequence of complex numbers \((c_n)_{n=0}^{\infty}\) and an \(R > 0\) such that the sequence \((c_n r^n)\) is bounded for all \(r < R\), we need to determine if the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). 1. **Boundedness Condition**: For each \(r < R\), there exists a constant \(M_r\) such that \(|c_n r^n| \leq M_r\) for all \(n\). This implies \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\). 2. **Comparison Test**: For any \(s\) such that \(|z - z_0| = s < R\), choose \(r\) such that \(s < r < R\). Then, we can write: \[ |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \leq M_r \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \] Since \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n\) is a convergent geometric series (as \(0 < \frac{s}{r} < 1\)), by the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} |c_n| s^n\) converges. 3. **Radius of Convergence**: Using the Cauchy-Hadamard theorem, the radius of convergence \(R'\) is given by \(1 / \limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n}\). Given \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\) for each \(r < R\), taking the lim sup as \(n \to \infty\) shows that \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/r\). As \(r\) approaches \(R\), this implies \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/R\), hence the radius of convergence \(R'\) is at least \(R\). Therefore, the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Let $(c_n)_{n = 0}^{\\infty}$ be a sequence of complex numbers. Suppose there exists an $R > 0$ such that the sequence $(c_nr^n)$ is bounded for all $r < R$. Does it follow that the power series $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}c_n(z-z_0)^n$ converges absolutely for all $z$ with $|z-z_0| < R$? Provide a justification for your answer. Reference Answer: Okay, let's tackle this problem. So, the question is about power series convergence. We have a sequence of complex numbers \( (c_n) \) and there's a given \( R > 0 \) such that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( c_n r^n \) is bounded. We need to determine if this implies that the power series \( \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n \) converges absolutely for all \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| < R \). First, let me recall some basic concepts about power series. The radius of convergence \( R \) of a power series is the value such that the series converges absolutely if \( |z - z_0| < R \) and diverges if \( |z - z_0| > R \). There are formulas to compute the radius of convergence, like the root test (Cauchy-Hadamard theorem) or the ratio test. The Cauchy-Hadamard theorem states that the radius of convergence \( R \) is given by \( 1/\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \). Alternatively, if the limit \( L = \lim_{n \to \infty} |c_{n+1}/c_n| \) exists, then \( R = 1/L \). But here, instead of being given information about the lim sup or the ratio of coefficients, we are told that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( (c_n r^n) \) is bounded. That is, for each \( r < R \), there exists some constant \( M_r \) such that \( |c_n r^n| \leq M_r \) for all \( n \). So the question is: Does this condition guarantee that the power series converges absolutely for all \( |z - z_0| < R \)? Hmm. Let's think. The given condition is that \( |c_n| r^n \leq M_r \), but that's just boundedness. Boundedness of the terms \( |c_n| r^n \) doesn't necessarily imply that their series converges. Then since |c_n| r^n <= M_r, then |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n (s/r)^n <= M_r (s/r)^n. Since the sum over n of (s/r)^n is a convergent geometric series, by comparison, the series sum |c_n| s^n converges. Hence, absolute convergence at z with |z - z0| = s < R. Since s is arbitrary, the series converges absolutely for all |z - z0| < R. Therefore, the answer is yes, it does follow. But let me check for possible counterexamples. Suppose someone says, "what if the sequence \( c_n r^n \) is bounded but doesn't go to zero?" However, even if \( c_n r^n \) is bounded, the terms \( c_n s^n \) for s < r would decay geometrically because \( |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n (s/r)^n \leq M_r (s/r)^n \), and since \( s/r < 1 \), the terms go to zero exponentially. So even if \( c_n r^n \) doesn't go to zero, when we take a smaller s, the terms \( c_n s^n \) do go to zero, and their series converges. Wait, but for the series \( \sum |c_n| s^n \), even if \( |c_n| r^n \leq M_r \), then \( |c_n| s^n \leq M_r (s/r)^n \), which is summable because it's a geometric series with ratio < 1. Therefore, the comparison test gives absolute convergence. Therefore, the answer should be yes, the power series converges absolutely for all |z - z0| < R. Another way to think about it: If for every r < R, c_n r^n is bounded, then the radius of convergence is at least R. Because if it were less than R, say the radius is ρ < R, then for r with ρ < r < R, the terms c_n r^n would be unbounded (since beyond the radius of convergence, the terms must be unbounded). But in our case, they are bounded, so the radius cannot be less than R. Therefore, the radius of convergence is at least R, hence the series converges absolutely inside |z - z0| < R. This seems solid. So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given a sequence of complex numbers \((c_n)_{n=0}^{\infty}\) and an \(R > 0\) such that the sequence \((c_n r^n)\) is bounded for all \(r < R\), we need to determine if the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). 1. **Boundedness Condition**: For each \(r < R\), there exists a constant \(M_r\) such that \(|c_n r^n| \leq M_r\) for all \(n\). This implies \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\). 2. **Comparison Test**: For any \(s\) such that \(|z - z_0| = s < R\), choose \(r\) such that \(s < r < R\). Then, we can write: \[ |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \leq M_r \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \] Since \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n\) is a convergent geometric series (as \(0 < \frac{s}{r} < 1\)), by the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} |c_n| s^n\) converges. 3. **Radius of Convergence**: Using the Cauchy-Hadamard theorem, the radius of convergence \(R'\) is given by \(1 / \limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n}\). Given \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\) for each \(r < R\), taking the lim sup as \(n \to \infty\) shows that \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/r\). As \(r\) approaches \(R\), this implies \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/R\), hence the radius of convergence \(R'\) is at least \(R\). Therefore, the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Let $(c_n)_{n = 0}^{\\infty}$ be a sequence of complex numbers. Suppose there exists an $R > 0$ such that the sequence $(c_nr^n)$ is bounded for all $r < R$. Does it follow that the power series $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}c_n(z-z_0)^n$ converges absolutely for all $z$ with $|z-z_0| < R$? Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Reference Answer: Okay, let's tackle this problem. So, the question is about power series convergence. We have a sequence of complex numbers \( (c_n) \) and there's a given \( R > 0 \) such that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( c_n r^n \) is bounded. We need to determine if this implies that the power series \( \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n \) converges absolutely for all \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| < R \). First, let me recall some basic concepts about power series. The radius of convergence \( R \) of a power series is the value such that the series converges absolutely if \( |z - z_0| < R \) and diverges if \( |z - z_0| > R \). There are formulas to compute the radius of convergence, like the root test (Cauchy-Hadamard theorem) or the ratio test. The Cauchy-Hadamard theorem states that the radius of convergence \( R \) is given by \( 1/\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \). Alternatively, if the limit \( L = \lim_{n \to \infty} |c_{n+1}/c_n| \) exists, then \( R = 1/L \). But here, instead of being given information about the lim sup or the ratio of coefficients, we are told that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( (c_n r^n) \) is bounded. That is, for each \( r < R \), there exists some constant \( M_r \) such that \( |c_n r^n| \leq M_r \) for all \( n \). So the question is: Does this condition guarantee that the power series converges absolutely for all \( |z - z_0| < R \)? Hmm. Let's think. Absolute convergence of the power series at a point \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| = r \) requires that \( \sum_{n=0}^\infty |c_n| r^n \) converges. The given condition is that \( |c_n| r^n \leq M_r \), but that's just boundedness. Boundedness of the terms \( |c_n| r^n \) doesn't necessarily imply that their series converges. For example, if \( |c_n| r^n \) is bounded but doesn't approach zero, then the series would diverge. However, in our case, even if \( |c_n| r^n \) is bounded, if it doesn't tend to zero, the series \( \sum |c_n| r^n \) would diverge because the terms don't approach zero. Therefore, the comparison test gives absolute convergence. Therefore, the answer should be yes, the power series converges absolutely for all |z - z0| < R. Another way to think about it: If for every r < R, c_n r^n is bounded, then the radius of convergence is at least R. Because if it were less than R, say the radius is ρ < R, then for r with ρ < r < R, the terms c_n r^n would be unbounded (since beyond the radius of convergence, the terms must be unbounded). But in our case, they are bounded, so the radius cannot be less than R. Therefore, the radius of convergence is at least R, hence the series converges absolutely inside |z - z0| < R. This seems solid. So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given a sequence of complex numbers \((c_n)_{n=0}^{\infty}\) and an \(R > 0\) such that the sequence \((c_n r^n)\) is bounded for all \(r < R\), we need to determine if the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). 1. **Boundedness Condition**: For each \(r < R\), there exists a constant \(M_r\) such that \(|c_n r^n| \leq M_r\) for all \(n\). This implies \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\). 2. **Comparison Test**: For any \(s\) such that \(|z - z_0| = s < R\), choose \(r\) such that \(s < r < R\). Then, we can write: \[ |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \leq M_r \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \] Since \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n\) is a convergent geometric series (as \(0 < \frac{s}{r} < 1\)), by the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} |c_n| s^n\) converges. 3. **Radius of Convergence**: Using the Cauchy-Hadamard theorem, the radius of convergence \(R'\) is given by \(1 / \limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n}\). Given \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\) for each \(r < R\), taking the lim sup as \(n \to \infty\) shows that \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/r\). As \(r\) approaches \(R\), this implies \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/R\), hence the radius of convergence \(R'\) is at least \(R\). Therefore, the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Let $(c_n)_{n = 0}^{\\infty}$ be a sequence of complex numbers. Suppose there exists an $R > 0$ such that the sequence $(c_nr^n)$ is bounded for all $r < R$. Does it follow that the power series $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}c_n(z-z_0)^n$ converges absolutely for all $z$ with $|z-z_0| < R$? Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes, it follows that the power series converges absolutely for all $z$ with $|z-z_0| < R$. \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, let's tackle this problem. So, the question is about power series convergence. We have a sequence of complex numbers \( (c_n) \) and there's a given \( R > 0 \) such that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( c_n r^n \) is bounded. We need to determine if this implies that the power series \( \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n \) converges absolutely for all \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| < R \). First, let me recall some basic concepts about power series. The radius of convergence \( R \) of a power series is the value such that the series converges absolutely if \( |z - z_0| < R \) and diverges if \( |z - z_0| > R \). There are formulas to compute the radius of convergence, like the root test (Cauchy-Hadamard theorem) or the ratio test. The Cauchy-Hadamard theorem states that the radius of convergence \( R \) is given by \( 1/\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \). Alternatively, if the limit \( L = \lim_{n \to \infty} |c_{n+1}/c_n| \) exists, then \( R = 1/L \). But here, instead of being given information about the lim sup or the ratio of coefficients, we are told that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( (c_n r^n) \) is bounded. That is, for each \( r < R \), there exists some constant \( M_r \) such that \( |c_n r^n| \leq M_r \) for all \( n \). So the question is: Does this condition guarantee that the power series converges absolutely for all \( |z - z_0| < R \)? Hmm. Let's think. Absolute convergence of the power series at a point \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| = r \) requires that \( \sum_{n=0}^\infty |c_n| r^n \) converges. The given condition is that \( |c_n| r^n \leq M_r \), but that's just boundedness. Boundedness of the terms \( |c_n| r^n \) doesn't necessarily imply that their series converges. For example, if \( |c_n| r^n \) is bounded but doesn't approach zero, then the series would diverge. However, in our case, even if \( |c_n| r^n \) is bounded, if it doesn't tend to zero, the series \( \sum |c_n| r^n \) would diverge because the terms don't approach zero. But wait, for convergence of the series, it's necessary (but not sufficient) that the terms approach zero. But the problem states that \( c_n r^n \) is bounded for all \( r < R \). So for each \( r < R \), there exists \( M_r \) such that \( |c_n| r^n \leq M_r \). However, to have convergence of the series \( \sum |c_n| r^n \), we need more than just boundedness. For example, take \( c_n = 1 \) for all \( n \). Then \( c_n r^n = r^n \), which is bounded since \( r < 1 \), but \( \sum r^n \) converges (it's a geometric series). Wait, in this case, even though \( c_n r^n \) is bounded, the series does converge. Hmm, but if \( c_n = 1 \), then the radius of convergence is 1, right? So in that case, the series converges inside the radius of convergence. But in this example, the boundedness of \( c_n r^n \) for each \( r < R \) (which is 1 here) does lead to absolute convergence. But maybe there's a different example where boundedness does not lead to convergence? Wait, let me consider another example. Suppose \( c_n = n \). Then the radius of convergence is \( 1 \), since \( \limsup |c_n|^{1/n} = 1 \). But for \( r < 1 \), \( |c_n r^n| = n r^n \). Is this bounded? For fixed \( r < 1 \), the sequence \( n r^n \) tends to zero as \( n \to \infty \), so it's bounded. Indeed, the maximum of \( n r^n \) occurs around \( n = -1/\ln r \), and it's finite. So even though \( c_n r^n = n r^n \) is bounded for each \( r < 1 \), the series \( \sum n r^n \) does converge because the terms go to zero exponentially. Wait, but does the boundedness of \( c_n r^n \) in this case actually hold? Let's check for fixed \( r < 1 \). Let's take \( r = 1/2 \). Then \( n (1/2)^n \). The sequence \( n (1/2)^n \) tends to zero, so it's bounded. Similarly, for any \( r < 1 \), \( n r^n \) tends to zero, so it is bounded. Therefore, the given condition is satisfied: for each \( r < R = 1 \), \( c_n r^n \) is bounded. And the series \( \sum n z^n \) does converge absolutely for \( |z| < 1 \). So here, the conclusion holds. Wait, maybe even if \( c_n \) grows polynomially, \( c_n r^n \) would still be bounded for each \( r < R \), and the series would converge because of the exponential decay from \( r^n \). But perhaps if \( c_n \) is allowed to grow faster? But if \( c_n r^n \) is bounded for each \( r < R \), then for each \( r \), there exists \( M_r \) with \( |c_n| \leq M_r / r^n \). Then, for \( |z - z_0| = s < R \), we can choose \( r \) such that \( s < r < R \). Then, \( |c_n| s^n \leq M_r (s/r)^n \). Since \( s/r < 1 \), the series \( \sum M_r (s/r)^n \) converges as a geometric series. Therefore, by comparison test, \( \sum |c_n| s^n \) converges. Therefore, absolute convergence at \( s \). Wait, this seems like a standard argument. So in fact, if for each \( r < R \), \( |c_n| r^n \) is bounded, then for any \( s < R \), we can take \( r \) between \( s \) and \( R \), so that \( |c_n| s^n \leq M_r (s/r)^n \), and since \( s/r < 1 \), the series converges. Therefore, absolute convergence follows. But the user's problem states that "the sequence \( (c_n r^n) \) is bounded for all \( r < R \)". So in other words, for each \( r < R \), \( \sup_n |c_n r^n| < \infty \). Then, for any \( s < R \), pick \( r \) such that \( s < r < R \). Then \( |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n (s/r)^n \leq M_r (s/r)^n \). Since \( \sum (s/r)^n \) is a convergent geometric series, by comparison test, \( \sum |c_n| s^n \) converges. Therefore, the power series converges absolutely for \( |z - z_0| < R \). Therefore, the answer should be yes, it does follow. This implies \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\). 2. **Comparison Test**: For any \(s\) such that \(|z - z_0| = s < R\), choose \(r\) such that \(s < r < R\). Then, we can write: \[ |c_n| s^n = |c_n| r^n \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \leq M_r \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n \] Since \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \left(\frac{s}{r}\right)^n\) is a convergent geometric series (as \(0 < \frac{s}{r} < 1\)), by the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} |c_n| s^n\) converges. 3. **Radius of Convergence**: Using the Cauchy-Hadamard theorem, the radius of convergence \(R'\) is given by \(1 / \limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n}\). Given \(|c_n| \leq M_r / r^n\) for each \(r < R\), taking the lim sup as \(n \to \infty\) shows that \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/r\). As \(r\) approaches \(R\), this implies \(\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \leq 1/R\), hence the radius of convergence \(R'\) is at least \(R\). Therefore, the power series \(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n\) converges absolutely for all \(z\) with \(|z - z_0| < R\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Let $(c_n)_{n = 0}^{\\infty}$ be a sequence of complex numbers. Suppose there exists an $R > 0$ such that the sequence $(c_nr^n)$ is bounded for all $r < R$. Does it follow that the power series $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}c_n(z-z_0)^n$ converges absolutely for all $z$ with $|z-z_0| < R$? Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes. \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, let's tackle this problem. So, the question is about power series convergence. We have a sequence of complex numbers \( (c_n) \) and there's a given \( R > 0 \) such that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( c_n r^n \) is bounded. We need to determine if this implies that the power series \( \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} c_n (z - z_0)^n \) converges absolutely for all \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| < R \). First, let me recall some basic concepts about power series. The radius of convergence \( R \) of a power series is the value such that the series converges absolutely if \( |z - z_0| < R \) and diverges if \( |z - z_0| > R \). There are formulas to compute the radius of convergence, like the root test (Cauchy-Hadamard theorem) or the ratio test. The Cauchy-Hadamard theorem states that the radius of convergence \( R \) is given by \( 1/\limsup_{n \to \infty} |c_n|^{1/n} \). Alternatively, if the limit \( L = \lim_{n \to \infty} |c_{n+1}/c_n| \) exists, then \( R = 1/L \). But here, instead of being given information about the lim sup or the ratio of coefficients, we are told that for every \( r < R \), the sequence \( (c_n r^n) \) is bounded. That is, for each \( r < R \), there exists some constant \( M_r \) such that \( |c_n r^n| \leq M_r \) for all \( n \). So the question is: Does this condition guarantee that the power series converges absolutely for all \( |z - z_0| < R \)? Hmm. Let's think. Absolute convergence of the power series at a point \( z \) with \( |z - z_0| = r \) requires that \( \sum_{n=0}^\infty |c_n| r^n \) converges. The given condition is that \( |c_n| r^n \leq M_r \), but that's just boundedness. Boundedness of the terms \( |c_n| r^n \) doesn't necessarily imply that their series converges. For example, if \( |c_n| r^n \) is bounded but doesn't approach zero, then the series would diverge. However, in our case, even if \( |c_n| r^n \) is bounded, if it doesn't tend to zero, the series \( \sum |c_n| r^n \) would diverge because the terms don't approach zero. But wait, for convergence of the series, it's necessary (but not sufficient) that the terms approach zero. But the problem states that \( c_n r^n \) is bounded for all \( r < R \). So for each \( r < R \), there exists \( M_r \) such that \( |c_n| r^n \leq M_r \). However, to have convergence of the series \( \sum |c_n| r^n \), we need more than just boundedness. For example, take \( c_n = 1 \) for all \( n \). Then \( c_n r^n = r^n \), which is bounded since \( r < 1 \), but \( \sum r^n \) converges (it's a geometric series). Wait, in this case, even though \( c_n r^n \) is bounded, the series does converge. Hmm, but if \( c_n = 1 \), then the radius of convergence is 1, right? So in that case, the series converges inside the radius of convergence. But in this example, the boundedness of \( c_n r^n \) for each \( r < R \) (which is 1 here) does lead to absolute convergence. But maybe there's a different example where boundedness does not lead to convergence? Wait, let me consider another example. Suppose \( c_n = n \). Then the radius of convergence is \( 1 \), since \( \limsup |c_n|^{1/n} = 1 \). But for \( r < 1 \), \( |c_n r^n| = n r^n \). Is this bounded? For fixed \( r < 1 \), the sequence \( n r^n \) tends to zero as \( n \to \infty \), so it's bounded. Indeed, the maximum of \( n r^n \) occurs around \( n = -1/\ln r \), and it's finite. So even though \( c_n r^n = n r^n \) is bounded for each \( r < 1 \), the series \( \sum n r^n \) does converge because the terms go to zero exponentially. Wait, but does the boundedness of \( c_n r^n \) in this case actually hold? Let's check for fixed \( r < 1 \). Let's take \( r = 1/2 \). Then \( n (1/2)^n \). Wait, maybe even if \( c_n \) grows polynomially, \( c_n r^n \) would still be bounded for each \( r < R \), and the series would converge because of the exponential decay from \( r^n \). But perhaps if \( c_n \) is allowed to grow faster? But if \( c_n r^n \) is bounded for each \( r < R \), then for each \( r \), there exists \( M_r \) with \( |c_n| \leq M_r / r^n \). Then, for \( |z - z_0| = s < R \), we can choose \( r \) such that \( s < r < R \). Then, \( |c_n| s^n \leq M_r (s/r)^n \). Since \( s/r < 1 \), the series \( \sum M_r (s/r)^n \) converges as a geometric series. Therefore, by comparison test, \( \sum |c_n| s^n \) converges. Therefore, absolute convergence at \( s \). Wait, this seems like a standard argument. Problem: A bookstore is deciding what price it should charge for a certain book. After research, the store finds that if the book's price is $p$ dollars (where $p \le 26$), then the number of books sold per month is $130-5p$. What price should the store charge to maximize its revenue? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: The bookstore should charge$10 \boxed{10}

Reference Answer: The store's revenue is given by: number of books sold $\times$ price of each book, or $p(130-5p)=130p-5p^2$. We want to maximize this expression by completing the square. We can factor out a $-5$ to get $-5(p^2-26p)$. To complete the square, we add $(26/2)^2=169$ inside the parenthesis and subtract $-5\cdot169=-845$ outside. We are left with the expression \[-5(p^2-26p+169)+845=-5(p-13)^2+845.\]Note that the $-5(p-13)^2$ term will always be nonpositive since the perfect square is always nonnegative. Thus, the revenue is maximized when $-5(p-13)^2$ equals 0, which is when $p=13$. Thus, the store should charge $\boxed{13}$ dollars for the book.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

What price should the store charge to maximize its revenue? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: The book maximizes its revenue when the number of books sold per month is maximized, or $130-5p=130$ (\boxed{26})

Reference Answer: The store's revenue is given by: number of books sold $\times$ price of each book, or $p(130-5p)=130p-5p^2$. We want to maximize this expression by completing the square. We can factor out a $-5$ to get $-5(p^2-26p)$. To complete the square, we add $(26/2)^2=169$ inside the parenthesis and subtract $-5\cdot169=-845$ outside. We are left with the expression \[-5(p^2-26p+169)+845=-5(p-13)^2+845.\]Note that the $-5(p-13)^2$ term will always be nonpositive since the perfect square is always nonnegative. Thus, the revenue is maximized when $-5(p-13)^2$ equals 0, which is when $p=13$. Thus, the store should charge $\boxed{13}$ dollars for the book.

Problem: A bookstore is deciding what price it should charge for a certain book. After research, the store finds that if the book's price is $p$ dollars (where $p \le 26$), then the number of books sold per month is $130-5p$. What price should the store charge to maximize its revenue? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: The revenue per month is $\$130-5p$, so the total revenue from selling$X$books is $(130-5p)X$. To maximize revenue, we want to maximize $(130-5p)X$. To do this, we want $130-5p$ to be as big as possible, which is when$5p=130$, or$5^{-1} \times 130=p$. We have$26^{-1} \times 130=22$, so$ \boxed{22}.

Reference Answer: The store's revenue is given by: number of books sold $\times$ price of each book, or $p(130-5p)=130p-5p^2$. We want to maximize this expression by completing the square. We can factor out a $-5$ to get $-5(p^2-26p)$. To complete the square, we add $(26/2)^2=169$ inside the parenthesis and subtract $-5\cdot169=-845$ outside. We are left with the expression \[-5(p^2-26p+169)+845=-5(p-13)^2+845.\]Note that the $-5(p-13)^2$ term will always be nonpositive since the perfect square is always nonnegative. Thus, the revenue is maximized when $-5(p-13)^2$ equals 0, which is when $p=13$. Thus, the store should charge $\boxed{13}$ dollars for the book.
